## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: nonlinear dynamics / classical mechanics
**Analysis**: This task models the acceleration of a nonlinear harmonic oscillator, where dv_dt depends on position x (restoring force), velocity v (damping), and possibly time t (external driving). Nonlinear oscillators feature polynomial or transcendental restoring/damping forces beyond the linear harmonic case.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Simple harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x (linear restoring force)
- Damped harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v (linear restoring + linear damping)
- Duffing oscillator (undriven): dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 (cubic nonlinear restoring force)
- Damped Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v (cubic restoring + linear damping)
- Driven Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * cos(c4 * t) (with periodic driving)
- Van der Pol oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * (1 - c2 * x**2) * v (nonlinear damping)
- Quintic nonlinearity: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * x**5 (higher-order restoring force)
- Quadratic-cubic nonlinearity: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 + c2 * x**3 (asymmetric potential)
- Nonlinear damping with driving: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * v * x**2 + c4 * sin(c5 * t)
- Velocity-dependent restoring: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v**2 * x (coupling between velocity and position)
- Driven damped oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * sin(c3 * t + c4) (linear driven-damped)
- Rayleigh oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v**3 (cubic velocity damping)
- Helmholtz oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 (quadratic nonlinearity)
- General polynomial oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 + c2 * x**3 + c3 * v + c4 * cos(c5 * t)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Odd-power restoring forces (x, x**3, x**5) → symmetric potential wells (Duffing-type systems)
- Even-power position terms (x**2) in restoring force → asymmetric potential wells breaking spatial symmetry
- Velocity-position coupling terms like v*x**2 → amplitude-dependent damping (Van der Pol type)
- Sinusoidal time dependence sin(c*t) or cos(c*t) → external periodic driving force
- Negative linear damping with positive cubic damping (c1*v + c2*v**3, opposite signs) → self-sustained limit cycle oscillations
- Sign of cubic coefficient relative to linear: c0*x + c1*x**3 with same sign → hardening spring; opposite sign → softening spring
- Absence of explicit time dependence → autonomous system (conservative or dissipative without driving)
- Products v*x → Coriolis-like or gyroscopic coupling terms in rotating frames

============================================================
GT Equivalence Verification (PO19)
============================================================
GT: -c0*beta*v - omega0**c1*x*exp(-Abs(x))
Candidate formulas: 50

# Analysis

The Ground Truth (GT) is:
$$f(x, v) = -0.59955v - 2.79523 \cdot x \cdot e^{-|x|}$$

The key challenge is whether any candidate can represent the term $x \cdot e^{-|x|}$. Let me note that $e^{-|x|}$ is a specific function that decreases exponentially with $|x|$.

## Key Observations

The GT has two terms:
1. A linear term in $v$: $-0.59955v$
2. A term $-2.79523 \cdot x \cdot e^{-|x|}$

The second term involves $e^{-|x|}$, which is **not** the same as $e^{cx^2}$ for any constant $c$ (the latter is Gaussian-shaped, while $e^{-|x|}$ has a cusp at 0 and different decay rate).

None of the candidates contain $e^{-|x|}$ directly. So the question is whether any combination of the available building blocks can exactly reproduce $x \cdot e^{-|x|}$ for all $x$.

The building blocks available across candidates include:
- $x$, $x^3$, $x^5$, $x^7$ (polynomial terms)
- $x \cdot e^{cx^2}$ (Gaussian-type terms)
- $x \cdot \log(c|x| + 1)$ (logarithmic terms)
- $x \cdot e^{cx^2} \cdot \log(c|x| + 1)$ (mixed terms)
- $x \cdot \log(c|x| + 1) \cdot \cos(cx)$ (oscillatory-log terms)
- $\sin$, $\cos$ terms
- $x/(c + x^2)$ (rational terms)
- $v$-dependent terms

**Can $x \cdot e^{cx^2} \cdot \log(c'|x| + 1)$ equal $x \cdot e^{-|x|}$?**

This would require $e^{cx^2} \cdot \log(c'|x| + 1) = e^{-|x|}$ for all $x$. The left side is smooth at $x=0$ (since $\log(c'|x|+1)$ behaves like $c'|x|$ near 0 and $e^{cx^2}$ is smooth), while $e^{-|x|}$ has a cusp in its derivative at $x=0$. However, when multiplied by $x$, both sides give functions that are odd and smooth-looking near 0. Still, the functional forms are fundamentally different.

For large $|x|$: $e^{-|x|}$ decays exponentially in $|x|$, while $e^{cx^2}$ either grows super-exponentially (if $c>0$) or decays super-exponentially (if $c<0$). No finite combination of these terms can match $e^{-|x|}$ decay.

**Can any finite sum of these building blocks reproduce $x \cdot e^{-|x|}$?**

No. The function $x \cdot e^{-|x|}$ has a specific exponential decay rate $\sim e^{-|x|}$ for large $|x|$. None of the available terms (polynomials, Gaussians, logs, products thereof) can produce this exact decay behavior. Polynomials grow, $e^{cx^2}$ has Gaussian decay/growth, $\log$ grows, and finite sums of these cannot reproduce pure exponential decay in $|x|$.

Even with many terms, you cannot exactly represent $e^{-|x|}$ as a finite combination of $e^{c_i x^2}$, polynomials, and logarithms.

## Conclusion

None of the 50 candidate formulas contain the building blocks necessary to exactly reproduce $x \cdot e^{-|x|}$. The available exponential terms are of the form $e^{cx^2}$ (Gaussian), not $e^{c|x|}$ (Laplacian). These are fundamentally different functional forms, and no parameter choice can bridge this gap.

I need to determine which candidates can be made equivalent to the ground truth:

**GT: -0.59955*v - 2.79523*x*exp(-|x|)**

The key function here is `x*exp(-|x|)`. I need to check if any combination of the basis functions in the candidates can exactly reproduce `x*exp(-|x|)`.

Let me first understand what basis functions appear in candidates:
- `x`, `x**3`, `x**5`, `x**7` (polynomials)
- `v`, `v*x`, `v*x**2`, `v**2*x`, `v**3` (velocity terms)
- `x*exp(c*x**2)` (Gaussian-type)
- `x*log(c*|x|+1)` (log terms)
- `x*exp(c*x**2)*log(c*|x|+1)` (mixed exp-log)
- `x*log(c*|x|+1)*cos(c*x)` (log-trig)
- `sin(...)`, `cos(...)` (trig terms)
- `x/(c+x**2)` (rational terms)
- Various other combinations

The GT requires:
1. A term proportional to `v` ✓ (most candidates have this)
2. A term equal to `x*exp(-|x|)` — this is the critical part

**Can `x*exp(-|x|)` be represented by these basis functions?**

`exp(-|x|)` is NOT the same as `exp(c*x**2)` for any constant c. The function `exp(-|x|)` has a cusp at x=0 and decays linearly in the exponent, while `exp(c*x**2)` is smooth and decays quadratically.

Can we use Taylor series or combinations? No — we need EXACT equivalence, not approximations.

Key question: Can `x*exp(-|x|)` be written as a finite combination of:
- polynomials in x
- `x*exp(c*x**2)` terms
- `x*log(c*|x|+1)` terms
- `x*exp(c*x**2)*log(c*|x|+1)` terms
- trig functions of x
- `x*log(c*|x|+1)*cos(c*x)` terms
- `x/(c+x**2)` rational terms

The answer is **no**. The function `exp(-|x|)` cannot be expressed as a finite sum of these basis functions. These are all fundamentally different function classes:

- `exp(-|x|)` has a non-analytic cusp at 0 (like `|x|` in the exponent)
- `exp(c*x**2)` is analytic everywhere
- `log(c*|x|+1)` grows logarithmically, not exponentially decaying
- Products of these don't produce `exp(-|x|)` behavior

None of these basis functions can combine to give `exp(-|x|)` exactly. The exponential decay `exp(-|x|)` is linearly independent from all Gaussian-type functions `exp(c*x**2)`, logarithmic functions, polynomial functions, trigonometric functions, and their products.

Therefore, **no candidate can match the GT**, because none contain the essential `exp(-|x|)` basis function (or anything that can produce it through constant choices).

Let me verify there's no hidden way: Could setting constants make unwanted terms vanish and somehow produce `x*exp(-|x|)`? No — every candidate's non-zero, non-v terms are built from the basis functions listed above, none of which equal `x*exp(-|x|)`.
